Email display settings

<h2>Email display settings</h2>



        

There is a 

setting

<a href="/hosting/settings">setting</a>

 to control how
        your email address appears on comments and issues that you post.

<p>There is a <a href="/hosting/settings">setting</a> to control how
        your email address appears on comments and issues that you post.</p>



        

Project members will always see your full email address.  By
        default, other users who visit the site will see an
        abbreviated version of your email address.

<p>Project members will always see your full email address.  By
        default, other users who visit the site will see an
        abbreviated version of your email address.</p>



        

If you do not wish your email address to be shared, there
        are other ways to 

get
        involved

<a href="http://www.chromium.org/getting-involved">get
        involved</a>

 in the community.  To report a problem when using
        the Chrome browser, you may use the "Report an issue..."  item
        on the "Help" menu.

<p>If you do not wish your email address to be shared, there
        are other ways to <a href="http://www.chromium.org/getting-involved">get
        involved</a> in the community.  To report a problem when using
        the Chrome browser, you may use the "Report an issue..."  item
        on the "Help" menu.</p>

This site hosts public issues in public projects

<h2>This site hosts public issues in public projects</h2>



        

Unlike our internal issue tracker, this site makes most
        issues public, unless the issue is labeled with a Restrict-View-*
        label, such as Restrict-View-Google.

<p>Unlike our internal issue tracker, this site makes most
        issues public, unless the issue is labeled with a Restrict-View-*
        label, such as Restrict-View-Google.</p>



        

Components are not used for permissions.  And, regardless of
        restriction labels, the issue reporter, owner,
        and Cc'd users may always view the issue.

<p>Components are not used for permissions.  And, regardless of
        restriction labels, the issue reporter, owner,
        and Cc'd users may always view the issue.</p>

Note:

<b>Note:</b>

        Description #

by higon...@gmail.com

<div class="select-container">
        <select aria-label="Description history menu" hidden="">

        Description #1 by higon...@gmail.com (Mar 30, 2021)
      </option>

in the function GeneratedCodeCache::WriteEntry, the buffer |data| is passed from renderer process, the function calculate the hash |checksum_key|



<span class="line"><!---->in the function GeneratedCodeCache::WriteEntry, the buffer |data| is passed from renderer process, the function calculate the hash |checksum_key|<!----></span>



<br>





firstly and then write the buffer to code cache backing store. if renderer modify the content of the buffer between calculation and write. the



<span class="line"><!---->firstly and then write the buffer to code cache backing store. if renderer modify the content of the buffer between calculation and write. the<!----></span>



<br>





checksum_key will mismatch with the content. so checksum_key can be mapped to any content.



<span class="line"><!---->checksum_key will mismatch with the content. so checksum_key can be mapped to any content.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





so an compromised renderer process can create an entry, whoes checksum_key will be used by a victim site, and the content of the code cache can be controlled by the compromised renderer process.



<span class="line"><!---->so an compromised renderer process can create an entry, whoes checksum_key will be used by a victim site, and the content of the code cache can be controlled by the compromised renderer process.<!----></span>



<br>





when the victim site load the code cache, any malicous code can be injected into the victim site, breaking the site isolation.



<span class="line"><!---->when the victim site load the code cache, any malicous code can be injected into the victim site, breaking the site isolation.<!----></span>

Since the GeneratedCodeCache also keys based on the origin requesting the resource, it seems like this is limited to one page within an origin attacking another. Assigning low severity since this seems to require both a compromised renderer and a very tight race condition.



<span class="line"><!---->Since the GeneratedCodeCache also keys based on the origin requesting the resource, it seems like this is limited to one page within an origin attacking another. Assigning low severity since this seems to require both a compromised renderer and a very tight race condition.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





Unfortunately there's no owners on the code cache itself, so triaging to top-level content/ OWNERs.



<span class="line"><!---->Unfortunately there's no owners on the code cache itself, so triaging to top-level content/ OWNERs.<!----></span>

Thanks for the report!



<span class="line"><!---->Thanks for the report!<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





To be clear, the GeneratedCodeCache is meant to only allow a process to write entries that are readable from its own origin_lock (which can be enforced at the site boundary).  Your description seems to talk about a time-of-check-to-time-of-use (i.e., double fetch) bug with the checksum, but I don't see how that would allow a different origin to read the compromised results.  Can you say more about how your PoC causes 127.0.0.1 to read from 127.0.0.2's code cache?  That would help us understand the severity, which might need to be raised.



<span class="line"><!---->To be clear, the GeneratedCodeCache is meant to only allow a process to write entries that are readable from its own origin_lock (which can be enforced at the site boundary).  Your description seems to talk about a time-of-check-to-time-of-use (i.e., double fetch) bug with the checksum, but I don't see how that would allow a different origin to read the compromised results.  Can you say more about how your PoC causes 127.0.0.1 to read from 127.0.0.2's code cache?  That would help us understand the severity, which might need to be raised.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





(It's also worth confirming whether it's specific to 127.0.0.* as a special case or if it applies to non-local cases as well.)



<span class="line"><!---->(It's also worth confirming whether it's specific to 127.0.0.* as a special case or if it applies to non-local cases as well.)<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





I haven't looked into the PoC yet, but I'm CC'ing mythria@, who worked on the Site Isolation support for GeneratedCodeCache in 



<span class="line"><!---->I haven't looked into the PoC yet, but I'm CC'ing mythria@, who worked on the Site Isolation support for GeneratedCodeCache in <!----></span>





issue 812168



<span class="line"><!---->issue 812168<!----></span>





.

If the code cache is small, it stores the cache in the form as following



<span class="line"><!---->If the code cache is small, it stores the cache in the form as following<!----></span>



<br>





key = "_key$url \n$site"



<span class="line"><!---->key = "_key$url \n$site"<!----></span>



<br>





value = the content of code cache



<span class="line"><!---->value = the content of code cache<!----></span>



<br>





If the code cache is very large(larger than |GetLargeDataLimit()|), it stores the cache in two levels, in the form as following



<span class="line"><!---->If the code cache is very large(larger than |GetLargeDataLimit()|), it stores the cache in two levels, in the form as following<!----></span>



<br>





first_level_key = "_key$url \n$site"



<span class="line"><!---->first_level_key = "_key$url \n$site"<!----></span>



<br>





first_level_value = the hash string of the code cache



<span class="line"><!---->first_level_value = the hash string of the code cache<!----></span>



<br>





second_level_key = the hash string of the code cache



<span class="line"><!---->second_level_key = the hash string of the code cache<!----></span>



<br>





second_level_value = the content of code cache



<span class="line"><!---->second_level_value = the content of code cache<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





GeneratedCodeCache only ensure isolating small cache from each other, if the cache code is large and the content of code cache is the same, it'll use the same entry



<span class="line"><!---->GeneratedCodeCache only ensure isolating small cache from each other, if the cache code is large and the content of code cache is the same, it'll use the same entry<!----></span>

Right, we de-duplicate very large entries by using SHA-256 hash of contents. So indeed two origins could share the same data as long as the content is the same. It is also possible to change the contents in such a way that the SHA-256 hash still remains the same. I thought this was highly unlikely and hence safe to use SHA-256 hash to de-duplicate the entries. 



<span class="line"><!---->Right, we de-duplicate very large entries by using SHA-256 hash of contents. So indeed two origins could share the same data as long as the content is the same. It is also possible to change the contents in such a way that the SHA-256 hash still remains the same. I thought this was highly unlikely and hence safe to use SHA-256 hash to de-duplicate the entries. <!----></span>



<br>



<br>





+bbudge@ who actually implemented this.



<span class="line"><!---->+bbudge@ who actually implemented this.<!----></span>

If the compromised renderer is modifying the shared memory of the Mojo::BigBuffer there are two problems:



<span class="line"><!---->If the compromised renderer is modifying the shared memory of the Mojo::BigBuffer there are two problems:<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





1) While the browser process is computing the checksum and writing the cached entry. This seems extremely difficult to get the timing right. We could fix this by copying the data in the browser process before computing the checksum, with very little impact on performance.



<span class="line"><!---->1) While the browser process is computing the checksum and writing the cached entry. This seems extremely difficult to get the timing right. We could fix this by copying the data in the browser process before computing the checksum, with very little impact on performance.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





2) While the browser process is fetching from the cache. This would allow the compromised renderer to get compiled code for some other resource/origin. This seems harmless, since the renderer is already compromised.



<span class="line"><!---->2) While the browser process is fetching from the cache. This would allow the compromised renderer to get compiled code for some other resource/origin. This seems harmless, since the renderer is already compromised.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





Re #17 the Finch experiment is only adjusting the double-key threshold, so we can't directly turn it off. If Finch can push a very large threshold though, we could effectively make it never de-duplicate entries.



<span class="line"><!---->Re #17 the Finch experiment is only adjusting the double-key threshold, so we can't directly turn it off. If Finch can push a very large threshold though, we could effectively make it never de-duplicate entries.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





I will check out the PoC now.



<span class="line"><!---->I will check out the PoC now.<!----></span>

I looked at the PoC more closely. We create a big buffer with shared memory and use mojo JS interface to send that data over. Since we still have access to buffer, this data could be modified, after the computation of the key thus installing the modified content which the victim will then retrieve. For proof of concept, the patch changes the generated code cache implementation to sleep right after the computation of key, and we modify the content in a timeout handler. I believe this could happen even without the sleep as long as render process has access to the mojo::BigBuffer. I am not that familiar with mojo::BigBuffer. I am a bit unsure if this is indeed an issue even when we don't use mojo js bindings. 



<span class="line"><!---->I looked at the PoC more closely. We create a big buffer with shared memory and use mojo JS interface to send that data over. Since we still have access to buffer, this data could be modified, after the computation of the key thus installing the modified content which the victim will then retrieve. For proof of concept, the patch changes the generated code cache implementation to sleep right after the computation of key, and we modify the content in a timeout handler. I believe this could happen even without the sleep as long as render process has access to the mojo::BigBuffer. I am not that familiar with mojo::BigBuffer. I am a bit unsure if this is indeed an issue even when we don't use mojo js bindings. <!----></span>



<br>



<br>





So the real issue here I think is if renderer is able to modify the contents of mojo::BigBuffer after requesting a write we could potentially corrupt the cached code.  We still need a way to reliably change the contents after the computation of key on the browser side but before the contents are written but is possible. Without de-duplication this wouldn't be a security concern since they wouldn't be reused across origins. With de-duplication we could potentially reuse across origins and hence becomes a security issue.



<span class="line"><!---->So the real issue here I think is if renderer is able to modify the contents of mojo::BigBuffer after requesting a write we could potentially corrupt the cached code.  We still need a way to reliably change the contents after the computation of key on the browser side but before the contents are written but is possible. Without de-duplication this wouldn't be a security concern since they wouldn't be reused across origins. With de-duplication we could potentially reuse across origins and hence becomes a security issue.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





Please disregard my comments about SHA-256 hash in 



<span class="line"><!---->Please disregard my comments about SHA-256 hash in <!----></span>





comment#16



<a class="undefined" target="_blank" href="#c16"><!---->comment#16<!----></a>





. My understanding wasn't correct then. If we indeed can change contents, any hash could cause the same problems.



<span class="line"><!---->. My understanding wasn't correct then. If we indeed can change contents, any hash could cause the same problems.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





higongguang@, Please let me know if my understanding is correct this time.



<span class="line"><!---->higongguang@, Please let me know if my understanding is correct this time.<!----></span>



<br>





 



<span class="line"><!----> <!----></span>



<br>





The thing I am still unclear though is  if it is really possible for renderer to update the contents of mojo::BigBuffer after it has requested a code cache write. As far as I understand, we do transfer the ownership of shared memory to the big buffer. I am not fully sure if we can update it from renderer process. 



<span class="line"><!---->The thing I am still unclear though is  if it is really possible for renderer to update the contents of mojo::BigBuffer after it has requested a code cache write. As far as I understand, we do transfer the ownership of shared memory to the big buffer. I am not fully sure if we can update it from renderer process. <!----></span>



<br>



<br>





kinuko/bbudge, Do you know if this is possible? If yes, then should we copy the data for renderer-browser boundary?



<span class="line"><!---->kinuko/bbudge, Do you know if this is possible? If yes, then should we copy the data for renderer-browser boundary?<!----></span>

<a class="undefined" target="_blank" href="#c20"><!---->#c20<!----></a>





 part (2) - I think this is not entirely correct. In the case of very large buffer, we have different cache layout. As per line 382:



<span class="line"><!----> part (2) - I think this is not entirely correct. In the case of very large buffer, we have different cache layout. As per line 382:<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





    // 3. Very Large



<span class="line"><!---->    // 3. Very Large<!----></span>

    // [stream0] response time, size, checksum



<span class="line"><!---->    // [stream0] response time, size, checksum<!----></span>



<br>





    // [stream1] <empty>



<span class="line"><!---->    // [stream1] &lt;empty&gt;<!----></span>

<br>

<span class="line"><!---->    // [stream1 (checksum key entry)] data<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





What we do in this case is to have *two* cache entries. One that takes the origin key we compute based on the process lock and stashes the checksum in the data. Then we write a second entry that uses the checksum as the lookup key and the actual script data.



<span class="line"><!---->What we do in this case is to have *two* cache entries. One that takes the origin key we compute based on the process lock and stashes the checksum in the data. Then we write a second entry that uses the checksum as the lookup key and the actual script data.<!----></span>



<br>





On lookups, we compute the origin key based on the same two inputs - the script URL and the process lock. Then we do another lookup based on the SHA hash that we get back. So here is a list of steps to exploit this as site isolation bypass as far as I understand it:



<span class="line"><!---->On lookups, we compute the origin key based on the same two inputs - the script URL and the process lock. Then we do another lookup based on the SHA hash that we get back. So here is a list of steps to exploit this as site isolation bypass as far as I understand it:<!----></span>



<br>



<br>

 loads script

.



<span class="line"><!---->.<!----></span>





 It writes two entries (v, f) -> sha, (sha) -> original content.



<span class="line"><!----> It writes two entries (v, f) -&gt; sha, (sha) -&gt; original content.<!----></span>

<span class="line"><!---->*<!----></span>

attacker.com

 loads script

 It writes two entries again - (a, f) -> sha, (sha) -> *altered content using this bug*



<span class="line"><!----> It writes two entries again - (a, f) -&gt; sha, (sha) -&gt; *altered content using this bug*<!----></span>

 gets reloaded or anoter page loads script



<span class="line"><!----> gets reloaded or anoter page loads script<!----></span>

 It does a lookup using (v, f) and gets back sha back. It then does a second lookup of (sha) and it gets back the altered content that the attacker cached.



<span class="line"><!----> It does a lookup using (v, f) and gets back sha back. It then does a second lookup of (sha) and it gets back the altered content that the attacker cached.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





The problem is that we effectively have content addressable cache for this case, which bypasses the origin protections we have in place. We can fix this in two ways - the proposed way of making a copy before computing the hash or we don't deduplicate across origins, which is what the current scheme is trying to do.



<span class="line"><!---->The problem is that we effectively have content addressable cache for this case, which bypasses the origin protections we have in place. We can fix this in two ways - the proposed way of making a copy before computing the hash or we don't deduplicate across origins, which is what the current scheme is trying to do.<!----></span>

I'm also curious about the steps in nasko@'s 



<span class="line"><!---->I'm also curious about the steps in nasko@'s <!----></span>





comment 30



<a class="undefined" target="_blank" href="#c30"><!---->comment 30<!----></a>





, where the victim writes a good entry to the content addressable cache, and then the attacker overwrites it with a bad entry at the same checksum key.  If that happens, is there a reason we allow entries in the content addressable cache to be overwritten?  Seems like a value shouldn't change for a given key, so maybe we could prevent changes?  (That said, I'm unclear if it's necessary for the victim to write the good entry first for the attack to work-- nasko@ mentioned it might be, but at first glance the PoC seems to have the attacker write the entry first.)

re c#35, Yes Site Isolation are fully launched in M72 and is fully enabled. I was only talking about de-duplication of the large entries in my earlier comment. As Bill clarified later, de-duplication feature is also enabled by default and the finch experiment is only changing the thresholds at which we trigger the de-duplication.



<span class="line"><!---->re c#35, Yes Site Isolation are fully launched in M72 and is fully enabled. I was only talking about de-duplication of the large entries in my earlier comment. As Bill clarified later, de-duplication feature is also enabled by default and the finch experiment is only changing the thresholds at which we trigger the de-duplication.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





re c#34, I believe we don't overwrite the data if there was already an entry associated with it [1]. Though we only skip writing it if lengths match. Actually lengths should match if there is a valid entry there. We do have DCHECKs later to check the entry was empty if the lengths don't match. In a release build possibly this DCHECKs don't trigger and we end up overwriting the entry. Though just preventing overwriting entries isn't sufficient to stop the attack.



<span class="line"><!---->re c#34, I believe we don't overwrite the data if there was already an entry associated with it [1]. Though we only skip writing it if lengths match. Actually lengths should match if there is a valid entry there. We do have DCHECKs later to check the entry was empty if the lengths don't match. In a release build possibly this DCHECKs don't trigger and we end up overwriting the entry. Though just preventing overwriting entries isn't sufficient to stop the attack.<!----></span>



<br>



<br>





In the scenario nasko@ mentioned, we can just skip step 1 and still get the attacker to force malicious code on victim sites. So, I think it isn't important that victim has to store first.  Copying the data before we compute the key should prevent these attacks. 



<span class="line"><!---->In the scenario nasko@ mentioned, we can just skip step 1 and still get the attacker to force malicious code on victim sites. So, I think it isn't important that victim has to store first.  Copying the data before we compute the key should prevent these attacks. <!----></span>



<br>



<br>

<a class="undefined" target="_blank" href="https://source.chromium.org/chromium/chromium/src/+/master:content/browser/code_cache/generated_code_cache.cc;l=577;drc=c72fbb03a13a55a11761912c96e5876de2cf15fb;bpv=1;bpt=1"><!---->https://source.chromium.org/chromium/chromium/src/+/master:content/browser/code_cache/generated_code_cache.cc;l=577;drc=c72fbb03a13a55a11761912c96e5876de2cf15fb;bpv=1;bpt=1<!----></a>

<br>

- Chrome:

*** Boilerplate reminders! ***



<span class="line"><!---->*** Boilerplate reminders! ***<!----></span>



<br>





Please do NOT publicly disclose details until a fix has been released to all our users. Early public disclosure may cancel the provisional reward. Also, please be considerate about disclosure when the bug affects a core library that may be used by other products. Please do NOT share this information with third parties who are not directly involved in fixing the bug. Doing so may cancel the provisional reward. Please be honest if you have already disclosed anything publicly or to third parties. Lastly, we understand that some of you are not interested in money. We offer the option to donate your reward to an eligible charity. If you prefer this option, let us know and we will also match your donation - subject to our discretion. Any rewards that are unclaimed after 12 months will be donated to a charity of our choosing.

          box-shadow: 2px 3px 8px 0px hsla(0, 0%, 0%, 0.3);
          border-spacing: 0;
          border-collapse: collapse;
          /* In the case when the autocomplete extends the
           * height of the viewport, we want to make sure
           * there's spacing. */
          margin-bottom: 1em;
        }

          display: block;
          min-width: 100px;
          max-height: 500px;
          overflow: auto;
        }
        .chops-autocomplete-container tr {
          cursor: pointer;
          transition: background 0.2s ease-in-out;
        }
        .chops-autocomplete-container tr[data-selected] {
          background: var(--chops-active-choice-bg);
          text-decoration: underline;
        }
        .chops-autocomplete-container td {
          padding: 0.25em 8px;
          white-space: nowrap;
        }
        .screenreader-hidden {
          clip: rect(1px, 1px, 1px, 1px);
          height: 1px;
          overflow: hidden;
          position: absolute;
          white-space: nowrap;
          width: 1px;
        }
